HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models      

# HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired  
Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models

<span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author"><span class="ltx_personname">Bernal Jiménez Gutiérrez<br class="ltx_break">The Ohio State University<br class="ltx_break">&amp;Yiheng Shu<br class="ltx_break">The Ohio State University<br class="ltx_break">Yu Gu<br class="ltx_break">The Ohio State University<br class="ltx_break">&amp;Michihiro Yasunaga<br class="ltx_break">Stanford University<br class="ltx_break">&amp;Yu Su<br class="ltx_break">The Ohio State University<br class="ltx_break"></span></span>

###### Abstract

In order to thrive in hostile and ever-changing natural environments, mammalian brains evolved to store large amounts of knowledge about the world and continually integrate new information while avoiding catastrophic forgetting. Despite their impressive accomplishments, large language models (LLMs), even with retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), still struggle to efficiently and effectively integrate a large amount of new experiences after pre-training. In this work, we introduce HippoRAG, a novel retrieval framework inspired by the hippocampal indexing theory of human long-term memory to enable deeper and more efficient knowledge integration over new experiences. HippoRAG synergistically orchestrates LLMs, knowledge graphs, and the Personalized PageRank algorithm to mimic the different roles of neocortex and hippocampus in human memory. We compare HippoRAG with existing RAG methods on multi-hop question answering (QA) and show that our method outperforms the state-of-the-art methods remarkably, by up to <math alttext="20" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="id1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="id1.1.m1.1a"><mn id="id1.1.m1.1.1" xref="id1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">20</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="id1.1.m1.1b"><cn id="id1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="id1.1.m1.1.1">20</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="id1.1.m1.1c">20</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="id1.1.m1.1d">20</annotation></semantics></math>%. Single-step retrieval with HippoRAG achieves comparable or better performance than iterative retrieval like IRCoT while being <math alttext="10" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="id2.2.m2.1"><semantics id="id2.2.m2.1a"><mn id="id2.2.m2.1.1" xref="id2.2.m2.1.1.cmml">10</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="id2.2.m2.1b"><cn id="id2.2.m2.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="id2.2.m2.1.1">10</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="id2.2.m2.1c">10</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="id2.2.m2.1d">10</annotation></semantics></math>\-<math alttext="20" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="id3.3.m3.1"><semantics id="id3.3.m3.1a"><mn id="id3.3.m3.1.1" xref="id3.3.m3.1.1.cmml">20</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="id3.3.m3.1b"><cn id="id3.3.m3.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="id3.3.m3.1.1">20</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="id3.3.m3.1c">20</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="id3.3.m3.1d">20</annotation></semantics></math> times cheaper and <math alttext="6" id="id4.4.m4.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="id4.4.m4.1.1">6</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="id4.4.m4.1c">6</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="id4.4.m4.1d">6</annotation></semantics></math>\-<math alttext="13" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="id5.5.m5.1"><semantics id="id5.5.m5.1a"><mn id="id5.5.m5.1.1" xref="id5.5.m5.1.1.cmml">13</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="id5.5.m5.1b"><cn id="id5.5.m5.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="id5.5.m5.1.1">13</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="id5.5.m5.1c">13</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="id5.5.m5.1d">13</annotation></semantics></math> times faster, and integrating HippoRAG into IRCoT brings further substantial gains. Finally, we show that our method can tackle new types of scenarios that are out of reach of existing methods.<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote1"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">1</span>Code and data are available at <a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://github.com/OSU-NLP-Group/HippoRAG" title="">https://github.com/OSU-NLP-Group/HippoRAG</a>.</span></span></span>

![Refer to caption](x1.png)

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 1: </span> <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S0.F1.9.1">Knowledge Integration &amp; RAG.</span> Tasks that require knowledge integration are particularly challenging for current RAG systems. In the above example, we want to find a <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S0.F1.10.2">Stanford</span> professor that does <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S0.F1.11.3">Alzheimer’s</span> research from a pool of passages describing potentially thousands <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S0.F1.12.4">Stanford</span> professors and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S0.F1.13.5">Alzheimer’s</span> researchers. Since current methods encode passages in isolation, they would struggle to identify <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S0.F1.14.6">Prof. Thomas</span> unless a passage mentions both characteristics at once. In contrast, most people familiar with this professor would remember him quickly due to our brain’s associative memory capabilities, thought to be driven by the index structure depicted in the C-shaped hippocampus above (in <span class="ltx_text" id="S0.F1.15.7" style="color:#5973A6;">blue</span>). Inspired by this mechanism, <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S0.F1.16.8">HippoRAG</span> allows LLMs to build and leverage a similar graph of associations to tackle knowledge integration tasks.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span> Introduction

Millions of years of evolution have led mammalian brains to develop the crucial ability to store large amounts of world knowledge and continuously integrate new experiences without losing previous ones. This exceptional long-term memory system eventually allows us humans to keep vast stores of continuously updating knowledge that forms the basis of our reasoning and decision making \[[19](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib19)\].

Despite the progress of large language models (LLMs) in recent years, such a continuously updating long-term memory is still conspicuously absent from current AI systems. Due in part to its ease of use and the limitations of other techniques such as model editing \[[46](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib46)\], retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) has become the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p2.1.1">de facto</span> solution for long-term memory in LLMs, allowing users to present new knowledge to a static model \[[36](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib36), [42](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib42), [66](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib66), [87](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib87)\].

However, current RAG methods are still unable to help LLMs perform tasks that require integrating new knowledge across passage boundaries since each new passage is encoded in isolation. Many important real-world tasks, such as scientific literature review, legal case briefing, and medical diagnosis, require knowledge integration across passages or documents. Although less complex, standard multi-hop question answering (QA) also requires integrating information between passages in a retrieval corpus. In order to solve such tasks, current RAG systems resort to using multiple retrieval and LLM generation steps iteratively to join disparate passages \[[64](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib64), [78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\]. Nevertheless, even perfectly executed multi-step RAG is still oftentimes insufficient to accomplish many scenarios of knowledge integration, as we illustrate in what we call <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p3.1.1">path-finding</span> multi-hop questions in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S0.F1 "Figure 1 ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

In contrast, our brains are capable of solving challenging knowledge integration tasks like these with relative ease. The hippocampal memory indexing theory \[[75](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib75)\], a well-established theory of human long-term memory, offers one plausible explanation for this remarkable ability. Teyler and Discenna \[[75](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib75)\] propose that our powerful context-based, continually updating memory relies on interactions between the neocortex, which processes and stores actual memory representations, and the C-shaped hippocampus, which holds the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p4.1.1">hippocampal index</span>, a set of interconnected indices which point to memory units on the neocortex and stores associations between them \[[19](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib19), [76](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib76)\].

In this work, we propose HippoRAG, a RAG framework that serves as a long-term memory for LLMs by mimicking this model of human memory. Our novel design first models the neocortex’s ability to process perceptual input by using an LLM to transform a corpus into a schemaless knowledge graph (KG) as our artificial hippocampal index. Given a new query, HippoRAG identifies the key concepts in the query and runs the Personalized PageRank (PPR) algorithm \[[30](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib30)\] on the KG, using the query concepts as the seeds, to integrate information across passages for retrieval. PPR enables HippoRAG to explore KG paths and identify relevant subgraphs, essentially performing multi-hop reasoning in a single retrieval step.

This capacity for <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p6.8.1">single-step multi-hop</span> retrieval yields strong performance improvements of around <math alttext="3" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.1.m1.1.1" xref="S1.p6.1.m1.1.1.cmml">3</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.1.m1.1.1">3</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.1.m1.1c">3</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.1.m1.1d">3</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="20" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.2.m2.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.2.m2.1.1" xref="S1.p6.2.m2.1.1.cmml">20</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.2.m2.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.2.m2.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.2.m2.1.1">20</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.2.m2.1c">20</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.2.m2.1d">20</annotation></semantics></math> points over current RAG methods \[[10](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib10), [35](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib35), [53](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib53), [70](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib70), [71](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib71)\] on two popular multi-hop QA benchmarks, MuSiQue \[[77](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib77)\] and 2WikiMultiHopQA \[[33](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib33)\]. Additionally, HippoRAG’s online retrieval process is <math alttext="10" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.3.m3.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.3.m3.1.1" xref="S1.p6.3.m3.1.1.cmml">10</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.3.m3.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.3.m3.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.3.m3.1.1">10</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.3.m3.1c">10</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.3.m3.1d">10</annotation></semantics></math> to <math alttext="30" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.4.m4.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.4.m4.1.1" xref="S1.p6.4.m4.1.1.cmml">30</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.4.m4.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.4.m4.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.4.m4.1.1">30</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.4.m4.1c">30</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.4.m4.1d">30</annotation></semantics></math> times cheaper and <math alttext="6" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.5.m5.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.5.m5.1.1" xref="S1.p6.5.m5.1.1.cmml">6</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.5.m5.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.5.m5.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.5.m5.1.1">6</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.5.m5.1c">6</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" retrieval methods like IRCoT \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\], while still achieving comparable performance. Furthermore, our approach can be combined with IRCoT to provide complementary gains of up to <math alttext="4" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.7.m7.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.7.m7.1.1" xref="S1.p6.7.m7.1.1.cmml">4</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.7.m7.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.7.m7.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.7.m7.1.1">4</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.7.m7.1c">4</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.7.m7.1d">4</annotation></semantics></math>% and <math alttext="20" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p6.8.m8.1"><semantics id="S1.p6.8.m8.1a"><mn id="S1.p6.8.m8.1.1" xref="S1.p6.8.m8.1.1.cmml">20</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p6.8.m8.1b"><cn id="S1.p6.8.m8.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S1.p6.8.m8.1.1">20</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S1.p6.8.m8.1c">20</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p6.8.m8.1d">20</annotation></semantics></math>% on the same datasets and even obtain improvements on HotpotQA, a less challenging multi-hop QA dataset. Finally, we provide a case study illustrating the limitations of current methods as well as our method’s potential on the previously discussed <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S1.p6.8.2">path-finding</span> multi-hop QA setting.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span> HippoRAG

In this section, we first give a brief overview of the hippocampal memory indexing theory, followed by how HippoRAG’s indexing and retrieval design was inspired by this theory, and finally offer a more detailed account of our methodology.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.1 </span> The Hippocampal Memory Indexing Theory

The hippocampal memory indexing theory \[[75](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib75)\] is a well-established theory that provides a functional description of the components and circuitry involved in human long-term memory. In this theory, Teyler and Discenna \[[75](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib75)\] propose that human long-term memory is composed of three components that work together to accomplish two main objectives: <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS1.p1.1.1">pattern separation</span>, which ensures that the representations of distinct perceptual experiences are unique, and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS1.p1.1.2">pattern completion</span>, which enables the retrieval of complete memories from partial stimuli \[[19](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib19), [76](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib76)\].

The theory suggests that pattern separation is primarily accomplished in the memory encoding process, which starts with the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.1">neocortex</span> receiving and processing perceptual stimuli into more easily manipulatable, likely higher-level, features, which are then routed through the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.2">parahippocampal regions</span> (PHR) to be indexed by the hippocampus. When they reach the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.3">hippocampus</span>, salient signals are included in the hippocampal index and associated with each other.

After the memory encoding process is completed, pattern completion drives the memory retrieval process whenever the hippocampus receives partial perceptual signals from the PHR pipeline. The hippocampus then leverages its context-dependent memory system, thought to be implemented through a densely connected network of neurons in the CA3 sub-region \[[76](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib76)\], to identify complete and relevant memories within the hippocampal index and route them back through the PHR for simulation in the neocortex. Thus, this complex process allows for new information to be integrated by changing only the hippocampal index instead of updating neocortical representations.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.2 </span> Overview

Our proposed approach, HippoRAG, is closely inspired by the process described above. As shown in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#footnote4 "footnote 4 ‣ Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Detailed Methodology ‣ 2 HippoRAG ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"), each component of our method corresponds to one of the three components of human long-term memory. A detailed example of the HippoRAG process can be found in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">A</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A1 "Appendix A HippoRAG Pipeline Example ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.1">Offline Indexing.</span> Our offline indexing phase, analogous to memory encoding, starts by leveraging a strong instruction-tuned <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.2">LLM</span>, our artificial neocortex, to extract knowledge graph (KG) triples. The KG is schemaless and this process is known as open information extraction (OpenIE) \[[3](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib3), [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib5), [60](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib60), [98](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib98)\]. This process extracts salient signals from passages in a retrieval corpus as discrete noun phrases rather than dense vector representations, allowing for more fine-grained pattern separation. It is therefore natural to define our artificial hippocampal index as this open <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.3">KG</span>, which is built on the whole retrieval corpus passage-by-passage. Finally, to connect both components as is done by the parahippocampal regions, we use off-the-shelf dense encoders fine-tuned for retrieval (<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.4">retrieval encoders</span>). These retrieval encoders provide additional edges between similar but not identical noun phrases within this KG to aid in downstream pattern completion.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS2.p3.1.1">Online Retrieval.</span> These same three components are then leveraged to perform online retrieval by mirroring the human brain’s memory retrieval process. Just as the hippocampus receives input processed through the neocortex and PHR, our LLM-based neocortex extracts a set of salient named entities from a query which we call <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS2.p3.1.2">query named entities</span>. These named entities are then linked to nodes in our KG based on the similarity determined by retrieval encoders; we refer to these selected nodes as <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS2.p3.1.3">query nodes</span>. Once the query nodes are chosen, they become the partial cues from which our synthetic hippocampus performs pattern completion. In the hippocampus, neural pathways between elements of the hippocampal index enable relevant neighborhoods to become activated and recalled upstream. To imitate this efficient graph search process, we leverage the Personalized PageRank (PPR) algorithm \[[30](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib30)\], a version of PageRank that distributes probability across a graph only through a set of user-defined source nodes. This constraint allows us to bias the PPR output only towards the set of query nodes, just as the hippocampus extracts associated signals from specific partial cues.<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote2"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">2</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">2</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">2</span>Intriguingly, some work in cognitive science has also found a correlation between human word recall and the output of the PageRank algorithm <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite">[<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib25" title="">25</a>]</cite>.</span></span></span> Finally, as is done when the hippocampal signal is sent upstream, we aggregate the output PPR node probability over the previously indexed passages and use that to rank them for retrieval.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.3 </span> Detailed Methodology

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS3.p1.13.1">Offline Indexing.</span> Our indexing process involves processing a set of passages <math alttext="P" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">P</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑃</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1c">P</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_P</annotation></semantics></math> using an instruction-tuned LLM <math alttext="L" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">L</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝐿</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1c">L</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_L</annotation></semantics></math> and a retrieval encoder <math alttext="M" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">M</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1.1">𝑀</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1c">M</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_M</annotation></semantics></math>. As seen in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#footnote4 "footnote 4 ‣ Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Detailed Methodology ‣ 2 HippoRAG ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") we first use <math alttext="L" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml">L</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1.1">𝐿</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1c">L</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.4.m4.1d">italic_L</annotation></semantics></math> to extract a set of noun phrase nodes <math alttext="N" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml">N</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1.1">𝑁</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1c">N</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.5.m5.1d">italic_N</annotation></semantics></math> and relation edges <math alttext="E" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml">E</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1.1">𝐸</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1c">E</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.6.m6.1d">italic_E</annotation></semantics></math> from each passage in <math alttext="P" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml">P</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1.1">𝑃</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1c">P</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.7.m7.1d">italic_P</annotation></semantics></math> via OpenIE. This process is done via 1-shot prompting of the LLM with the prompts shown in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">I</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A9 "Appendix I LLM Prompts ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"). Specifically, we first extract a set of named entities from each passage. We then add the named entities to the OpenIE prompt to extract the final triples, which also contain concepts (noun phrases) beyond named entities. We find that this two-step prompt configuration leads to an appropriate balance between generality and bias towards named entities. Finally, we use <math alttext="M" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml">M</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1.1">𝑀</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1c">M</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p1.8.m8.1d">italic_M</annotation></semantics></math> to add the extra set of <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p1.13.2">synonymy</span> relations <math alttext="E^{\prime}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.9.m9.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.9.m9.1d">italic_E start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ′ end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> discussed above when the cosine similarity between two entity representations in <math alttext="N" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1.1.cmml">N</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.10.m10.1.1">𝑁</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1.1.cmml">τ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p1.11.m11.1.1">𝜏</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" As stated above, this introduces more edges to our hippocampal index and allows for more effective pattern completion. This indexing process defines a <math alttext="|N|\times|P|" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p1.12.m12.2"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p1.12.m12.2a"><mrow id="S2.SS3.p1.12.m12.2.3" noun phrase in the KG appears in each original passage.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS3.p2.10.1">Online Retrieval.</span> During the retrieval process, we prompt <math alttext="L" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" These named entities <math alttext="C_{q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1a"><msub id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.2.cmml">C</mi><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.3.cmml">q</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.2">𝐶</ci><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1.1.3">𝑞</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1c">C_{q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p2.4.m4.1d">italic_C start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> from the query are then encoded by the same retrieval encoder <math alttext="M" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1.1.cmml">M</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1.1">𝑀</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1c">M</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p2.5.m5.1d">italic_M</annotation></semantics></math>. )</annotation></semantics></math>, represented as the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p2.10.4">Stanford</span> logo and the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p2.10.5">Alzheimer’s</span> purple ribbon symbol in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#footnote4 "footnote 4 ‣ Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Detailed Methodology ‣ 2 HippoRAG ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

![Refer to caption](x2.png)

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 2: </span> <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.F2.4.1">Detailed HippoRAG Methodology.</span> We model the three components of human long-term memory to mimic its pattern separation and completion functions. For offline indexing <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.F2.5.2">(Middle)</span>, we use an LLM to process passages into open KG triples, which are then added to our artificial hippocampal index, while our synthetic parahippocampal regions (PHR) detect synonymy. In the example above, triples involving Professor Thomas are extracted and integrated into the KG. For online retrieval <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.F2.6.3">(Bottom)</span>, our LLM neocortex extracts named entities from a query while our parahippocampal retrieval encoders link them to our hippocampal index. We then leverage the Personalized PageRank algorithm to enable context-based retrieval and extract Professor Thomas.<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote4"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">4</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">4</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">4</span>Many details around the hippocampal memory indexing theory are omitted from this study for simplicity. We encourage interested reader to follow the references in §<a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S2.SS1" title="2.1 The Hippocampal Memory Indexing Theory ‣ 2 HippoRAG ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2.1</span></a> for more.</span></span></span>

After the query nodes <math alttext="R_{q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">R</mi><mi id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">q</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1c">R_{q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1d">italic_R start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> are found, we run the PPR algorithm over the hippocampal index, i.e., a KG with <math alttext="|N|" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.2.2"><abs id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.2.2.1"></abs><ci id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.1.cmml" This allows probability mass to be distributed to nodes that are primarily in the (joint) neighborhood of the query nodes, such as <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p3.10.1">Professor Thomas</span>, and contribute to eventual retrieval. use for retrieval.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.1">Node Specificity.</span> We introduce node specificity as a neurobiologically plausible way to further improve retrieval. It is well known that global signals for word importance, like inverse document frequency (IDF), can improve information retrieval. However, in order for our brain to leverage IDF for retrieval, the number of total “passages” encoded would need to be aggregated with all node activations before memory retrieval is complete. While simple for normal computers, this process would require activating connections between an aggregator neuron and all nodes in the hippocampal index every time retrieval occurs, likely introducing prohibitive computational overhead. Given these constraints, we propose <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.2">node specificity</span> as an alternative IDF signal which requires only local signals and is thus more neurobiologically plausible. We define the node specificity of node <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml">i</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1.1">𝑖</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1c">i</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.1d">italic_i</annotation></semantics></math> as <math encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.2.m2.1c">s_{i}=|P_{i}|^{-1}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.2.m2.1d">italic_s start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = | italic_P start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT | start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT - 1 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>, where <math alttext="P_{i}" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1c">P_{i}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1d">italic_P start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> is the set of passages in <math alttext="P" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.4.m4.1.1" encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.4.m4.1c">P</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.4.m4.1d">italic_P</annotation></semantics></math> from which node <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1.1.cmml">i</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1.1">𝑖</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1c">i</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.5.m5.1d">italic_i</annotation></semantics></math> was extracted, information that is already available at each node. Node specificity is used in retrieval by multiplying each query node probability <math alttext="\vv{n}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.cmml"><merror class="ltx_ERROR undefined undefined" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2b.cmml"><mtext id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2a" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2b.cmml">\vv</mtext></merror><mo id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.3.cmml">n</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1"><times id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2b.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2"><merror class="ltx_ERROR undefined undefined" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2"><mtext id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2a.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.2">\vv</mtext></merror></ci><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1.1.3">𝑛</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1c">\vv{n}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.6.m6.1d">italic_n</annotation></semantics></math> with <math alttext="s_{i}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1a"><msub id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.2.cmml">s</mi><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.3.cmml">i</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.2">𝑠</ci><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1.1.3">𝑖</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1c">s_{i}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.m7.1d">italic_s start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> before PPR; this allows us to modulate each of their neighborhood’s probability as well as their own. We illustrate node specificity in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#footnote4 "footnote 4 ‣ Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Detailed Methodology ‣ 2 HippoRAG ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") through relative symbol size: the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.3">Stanford</span> logo grows larger than the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p4.7.4">Alzheimer’s</span> symbol since it appears in fewer documents.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span> Experimental Setup

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.1 </span> Datasets

We evaluate our method’s retrieval capabilities primarily on two challenging multi-hop QA benchmarks, <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.1">MuSiQue</span> (answerable) \[[77](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib77)\] and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.2">2WikiMultiHopQA</span> \[[33](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib33)\]. For completeness, we also include the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.3">HotpotQA</span> \[[89](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib89)\] dataset even though it has been found to be a much weaker test for multi-hop reasoning due to many spurious signals \[[77](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib77)\], as we also show in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">B</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A2 "Appendix B Dataset Comparison ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"). To limit the experimental cost, we extract <math alttext="1,000" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2"><semantics id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2a"><mrow id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.4"><mn id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1" xref="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.3.cmml">1</mn><mo id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.4.1" xref="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.3.cmml">,</mo><mn id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2" xref="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.3.cmml">000</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2b"><cn id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2.2.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1">1000</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2c">1,000</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS1.p1.1.m1.2d">1 , 000</annotation></semantics></math> questions from each validation set as done in previous work \[[63](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib63), [78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\]. In order to create a more realistic retrieval setting, we follow IRCoT \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\] and collect all candidate passages (including supporting and distractor passages) from our selected questions and form a retrieval corpus for each dataset. The details of these datasets are shown in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S3.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 3.1 Datasets ‣ 3 Experimental Setup ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.2 </span> Baselines

We compare against several strong and widely used retrieval methods: <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.1">BM25</span> \[[69](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib69)\], <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.2">Contriever</span> \[[35](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib35)\], <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.3">GTR</span> \[[53](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib53)\] and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.4">ColBERTv2</span> \[[70](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib70)\]. Additionally, we compare against two recent LLM-augmented baselines: <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.5">Propositionizer</span> \[[10](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib10)\], which rewrites passages into propositions, and <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.6">RAPTOR</span> \[[71](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib71)\], which constructs summary nodes to ease retrieval from long documents. In addition to the single-step retrieval methods above, we also include the multi-step retrieval method <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS2.p1.1.7">IRCoT</span> \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\] as a baseline.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.3 </span> Metrics

We report retrieval and QA performance on the datasets above using recall@2 and recall@5 (R@2 and R@5 below) for retrieval and exact match (EM) and F1 scores for QA performance.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.4 </span> Implementation Details

By default, we use <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter" id="S3.SS4.p1.7.1">GPT-3.5-turbo-1106</span> \[[55](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib55)\] with temperature of <math alttext="0" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">0</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.SS4.p1.1.m1.1.1">0</cn></annotation-xml></semantics></math> as our LLM <math alttext="L" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">L</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝐿</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1c">L</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_L</annotation></semantics></math> and Contriever \[[35](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib35)\] or ColBERTv2 \[[70](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib70)\] as our retriever <math alttext="M" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">M</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1.1">𝑀</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1c">M</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_M</annotation></semantics></math>. We use <math alttext="100" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1a"><mn id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml">100</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1b"><cn id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1.1">100</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1c">100</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.4.m4.1d">100</annotation></semantics></math> examples from MuSiQue’s training data to tune HippoRAG’s two hyperparameters: the synonymy threshold <math alttext="\tau" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml">τ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1.1">𝜏</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1c">\tau</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.5.m5.1d">italic_τ</annotation></semantics></math> at <math alttext="0.8" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1a"><mn id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml">0.8</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1b"><cn id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1.1">0.8</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1c">0.8</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.6.m6.1d">0.8</annotation></semantics></math> and the PPR damping factor at <math alttext="0.5" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1a"><mn id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1.1" xref="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml">0.5</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1b"><cn id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1.1">0.5</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1c">0.5</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS4.p1.7.m7.1d">0.5</annotation></semantics></math>, which determines the probability that PPR will restart a random walk from the query nodes instead of continuing to explore the graph. Generally, we find that HippoRAG’s performance is rather robust to its hyperparameters. More implementation details can be found in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">H</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A8 "Appendix H Implementation Details & Compute Requirements ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">4 </span> Results

We present our retrieval and QA experimental results below. Given that our method indirectly affects QA performance, we report QA results on our best-performing retrieval backbone ColBERTv2 \[[70](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib70)\]. However, we report retrieval results for several strong single-step and multi-step retrieval techniques.

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 2: </span> <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.T2.84.1">Single-step retrieval performance.</span> HippoRAG outperforms all baselines on MuSiQue and 2WikiMultiHopQA and achieves comparable performance on the less challenging HotpotQA dataset.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.T2.80.81.1.3.1" style="font-size:90%;">2Wiki</span>

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.T2.80.81.1.4.1" style="font-size:90%;">HotpotQA</span>

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.T2.80.81.1.5.1" style="font-size:90%;">Average</span>

<span class="ltx_text" id="S4.T2.80.82.2.2.1" style="font-size:90%;">R@2</span>

<span class="ltx_text" id="S4.T2.80.82.2.3.1" style="font-size:90%;">R@5</span>

<span class="ltx_text" id="S4.T2.80.82.2.4.1" style="font-size:90%;">R@2</span>

<span class="ltx_text" id="S4.T2.80.82.2.5.1" style="font-size:90%;">R@5</span>

<math alttext="\mathbf{78.2}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1"><semantics id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1a"><mn class="ltx_mathvariant_bold" id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1.1" mathsize="90%" mathvariant="bold" xref="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1.1.cmml">78.2</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1b"><cn id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1.1">78.2</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1c">\mathbf{78.2}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.T3.40.40.8.m1.1d">bold_78.2</annotation></semantics></math>

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.p2.3.1">Single-Step Retrieval Results.</span> As seen in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S4.T2 "Table 2 ‣ 4 Results ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"), HippoRAG outperforms all other methods, including recent LLM-augmented baselines such as Propositionizer and RAPTOR, on our main datasets, MuSiQue and 2WikiMultiHopQA, while achieving competitive performance on HotpotQA. We notice an impressive improvement of <math alttext="11" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S4.p2.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S4.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S4.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml">11</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p2.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S4.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p2.1.m1.1.1">11</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p2.1.m1.1c">11</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p2.1.m1.1d">11</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="20" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p2.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S4.p2.2.m2.1a"><mn id="S4.p2.2.m2.1.1" xref="S4.p2.2.m2.1.1.cmml">20</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p2.2.m2.1b"><cn id="S4.p2.2.m2.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p2.2.m2.1.1">20</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p2.2.m2.1c">20</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p2.2.m2.1d">20</annotation></semantics></math>% for R@2 and R@5 on 2WikiMultiHopQA and around <math alttext="3" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p2.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S4.p2.3.m3.1a"><mn id="S4.p2.3.m3.1.1" xref="S4.p2.3.m3.1.1.cmml">3</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p2.3.m3.1b"><cn id="S4.p2.3.m3.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p2.3.m3.1.1">3</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p2.3.m3.1c">3</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p2.3.m3.1d">3</annotation></semantics></math>% on MuSiQue. This difference can be partially explained by 2WikiMultiHopQA’s entity-centric design, which is particularly well-suited for HippoRAG. Our lower performance on HotpotQA is mainly due to its lower knowledge integration requirements, as explained in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">B</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A2 "Appendix B Dataset Comparison ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"), as well as a due to a concept-context tradeoff which we alleviate with an ensembling technique described in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">F.2</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A6.SS2 "F.2 Concepts vs. Context Tradeoff ‣ Appendix F Error Analysis ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.p3.3.1">Multi-Step Retrieval Results.</span> For multi-step or iterative retrieval, our experiments in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S4.T3 "Table 3 ‣ 4 Results ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") demonstrate that IRCoT \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib78)\] and HippoRAG are complementary. Using HippoRAG as the retriever for IRCoT continues to bring R@5 improvements of around <math alttext="4" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p3.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S4.p3.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S4.p3.1.m1.1.1" xref="S4.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml">4</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p3.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S4.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p3.1.m1.1.1">4</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p3.1.m1.1c">4</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p3.1.m1.1d">4</annotation></semantics></math>% for MuSiQue, <math alttext="18" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p3.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S4.p3.2.m2.1a"><mn id="S4.p3.2.m2.1.1" xref="S4.p3.2.m2.1.1.cmml">18</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p3.2.m2.1b"><cn id="S4.p3.2.m2.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p3.2.m2.1.1">18</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p3.2.m2.1c">18</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p3.2.m2.1d">18</annotation></semantics></math>% for 2WikiMultiHopQA and an additional <math alttext="1" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p3.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S4.p3.3.m3.1a"><mn id="S4.p3.3.m3.1.1" xref="S4.p3.3.m3.1.1.cmml">1</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p3.3.m3.1b"><cn id="S4.p3.3.m3.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p3.3.m3.1.1">1</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p3.3.m3.1c">1</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p3.3.m3.1d">1</annotation></semantics></math>% on HotpotQA.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.p4.7.1">Question Answering Results.</span> We report QA results for HippoRAG, the strongest retrieval baselines, ColBERTv2 and IRCoT, as well as IRCoT using HippoRAG as a retriever in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S4.T4 "Table 4 ‣ 4 Results ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"). As expected, improved retrieval performance in both single and multi-step settings leads to strong overall improvements of up to <math alttext="3" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p4.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S4.p4.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S4.p4.1.m1.1.1" xref="S4.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml">3</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p4.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S4.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p4.1.m1.1.1">3</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p4.1.m1.1c">3</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p4.1.m1.1d">3</annotation></semantics></math>%, <math alttext="17" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p4.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S4.p4.2.m2.1a"><mn id="S4.p4.2.m2.1.1" xref="S4.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml">17</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p4.2.m2.1b"><cn id="S4.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p4.2.m2.1.1">17</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p4.2.m2.1c">17</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p4.2.m2.1d">17</annotation></semantics></math>% and <math alttext="1" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p4.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S4.p4.3.m3.1a"><mn id="S4.p4.3.m3.1.1" xref="S4.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml">1</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S4.p4.3.m3.1b"><cn id="S4.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml" type="integer" xref="S4.p4.3.m3.1.1">1</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S4.p4.3.m3.1c">1</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S4.p4.3.m3.1d">1</annotation></semantics></math>% F1 scores on MuSiQue, 2WikiMultiHopQA and HotpotQA respectively using the same QA reader. Notably, single-step HippoRAG is on par or outperforms IRCoT while being <math alttext="10" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S4.p4.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S4.p4.4.m4.1a"><mn id="S4.p4.4.m4.1.1" ## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">5 </span> Discussions

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.1 </span> What Makes HippoRAG Work?

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 5: </span> <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.T5.70.1">Dissecting HippoRAG.</span> To understand what makes it work well, we replace its OpenIE module and PPR with plausible alternatives and ablate node specificity and synonymy-based edges.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.SS1.p1.1.1">OpenIE Alternatives.</span> To determine if using a closed model like GPT-3.5 is essential to retain our performance improvements, we replace it with an end-to-end OpenIE model REBEL \[[34](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib34)\] as well as the 8B and 70B instruction-tuned versions of Llama-3.1, a class of strong open-weight LLMs \[[1](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib1)\]. As shown in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 5.1 What Makes HippoRAG Work? ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") row 2, building our KG using REBEL results in large performance drops, underscoring the importance of LLM flexibility. Specifically, GPT-3.5 produces twice as many triples as REBEL, indicating its bias against producing triples with general concepts and leaving many useful associations behind.

In terms of open-weight LLMs, Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 5.1 What Makes HippoRAG Work? ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") (rows 3-4) shows that the performance of Llama-3.1-8B is competitive with GPT-3.5 in all datasets except for 2Wiki, where performance drops substantially. Nevertheless, the stronger 70B counterpart outperforms GPT-3.5 in two out of three datasets and is still competitive in 2Wiki. The strong performance of Llama-3.1-70B and the comparable performance of even the 8B model is encouraging since it offers a cheaper alternative for indexing over large corpora. The graph statistics for these OpenIE alternatives can be found in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">C</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A3 "Appendix C Ablation Statistics ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

To understand the relationship between OpenIE and retrieval performance more deeply, we extract 239 gold triples from 20 examples from the MuSiQue training set. We then perform a small-scale intrinsic evaluation using the CaRB \[[6](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#bib.bib6)\] framework for OpenIE. We find that both Llama-3.1-Instruct models underperform GPT-3.5 slightly on this intrinsic evaluation but all LLMs vastly outperform REBEL. More details about this evaluation experiments can be found in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">D</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A4 "Appendix D Intrinsic OpenIE Evaluation ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models").

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.1">PPR Alternatives.</span> As shown in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 5.1 What Makes HippoRAG Work? ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") (rows 5-6), to examine how much of our results are due to the strength of PPR, we replace the PPR output with the query node probability <math alttext="\vv{n}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1a"><mrow id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><merror class="ltx_ERROR undefined undefined" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2b.cmml"><mtext id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2a" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2b.cmml">\vv</mtext></merror><mo id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">n</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1"><times id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.1"></times><ci id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2b.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2"><merror class="ltx_ERROR undefined undefined" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2"><mtext id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2a.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2">\vv</mtext></merror></ci><ci id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3">𝑛</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1c">\vv{n}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.SS1.p4.1.m1.1d">italic_n</annotation></semantics></math> multiplied by node specificity values (row 5) and a version of this that also distributes a small amount of probability to the direct neighbors of each query node (row 6). First, we find that PPR is a much more effective method for including associations for retrieval on all three datasets compared to both simple baselines. It is interesting to note that adding the neighborhood of <math alttext="R_{q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1a"><msub id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">R</mi><mi id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">q</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2">𝑅</ci><ci id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3">𝑞</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1c">R_{q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.SS1.p4.2.m2.1d">italic_R start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> nodes without PPR leads to worse performance than only using the query nodes themselves.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.SS1.p5.1.1">Ablations.</span> As seen in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 5.1 What Makes HippoRAG Work? ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") (rows 7-8), node specificity obtains considerable improvements on MuSiQue and HotpotQA and yields almost no change in 2WikiMultiHopQA. This is likely because 2WikiMultiHopQA relies on named entities with little differences in terms of term weighting. In contrast, synonymy edges have the largest effect on 2WikiMultiHopQA, suggesting that noisy entity standardization is useful when most relevant concepts are named entities, and improvements to synonymy detection could lead to stronger performance in other datasets.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.2 </span> HippoRAG’s Advantage: Single-Step Multi-Hop Retrieval

A major advantage of HippoRAG over conventional RAG methods in multi-hop QA is its ability to <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.SS2.p1.5.1">perform multi-hop retrieval in a single step</span>. We demonstrate this by measuring the percentage of queries where <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.SS2.p1.5.2">all</span> the supporting passages are retrieved successfully, a feat that can only be accomplished through successful multi-hop reasoning. Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">6</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T6 "Table 6 ‣ 5.2 HippoRAG’s Advantage: Single-Step Multi-Hop Retrieval ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models") below shows that the gap between our method and ColBERTv2, using the top-<math alttext="5" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" <math alttext="37.4" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1"><semantics id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1a"><mn id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1.1" mathsize="90%" xref="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1.1.cmml">37.4</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1b"><cn id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1.1">37.4</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1c">37.4</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.T6.8.8.8.m1.1d">37.4</annotation></semantics></math>

<span class="ltx_text" id="S5.T6.16.16.9.1" style="font-size:90%;">HippoRAG</span>

<math alttext="10.2" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1a"><mn id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1.1" mathsize="90%" xref="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1.1.cmml">10.2</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1b"><cn id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1.1">10.2</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1c">10.2</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.T6.9.9.1.m1.1d">10.2</annotation></semantics></math>

<math alttext="29.8" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1"><semantics id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1a"><mn id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1.1" mathsize="90%" xref="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1.1.cmml">29.8</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1b"><cn id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1.1">29.8</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1c">29.8</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.T6.15.15.7.m1.1d">29.8</annotation></semantics></math>

<math alttext="52.0" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1"><semantics id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1a"><mn id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1.1" mathsize="90%" xref="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1.1.cmml">52.0</mn><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1b"><cn id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1.1.cmml" type="float" xref="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1.1">52.0</cn></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1c">52.0</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S5.T6.16.16.8.m1.1d">52.0</annotation></semantics></math>

We further illustrate HippoRAG’s unique <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.SS2.p2.4.1">single-step multi-hop retrieval</span> ability through the first example in Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">7</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#S5.T7 "Table 7 ‣ 5.2 HippoRAG’s Advantage: Single-Step Multi-Hop Retrieval ‣ 5 Discussions ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"). In this example, even though <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.SS2.p2.4.2">Alhandra</span> was not mentioned in <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.SS2.p2.4.3">Vila de Xira’s</span> passage, HippoRAG can directly leverage Vila de Xira’s connection to Alhandra as his place of birth to determine its importance, something that standard RAG methods would be unable to do directly. Additionally, even though IRCoT can also solve this multi-hop retrieval problem, as shown in Appendix [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">G</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2405.14831v3#A7 "Appendix G Cost and Efficiency Comparison ‣ HippoRAG: Neurobiologically Inspired Long-Term Memory for Large Language Models"), it is <math alttext="10" class="ltx_Math" display="inline"
